## Domain Knowledge (LLM-generated)
**Domain**: nonlinear oscillator dynamics
**Analysis**: This is a nonlinear harmonic oscillator where acceleration depends on position, velocity, and possibly time. The acceleration equation represents Newton's second law with nonlinear restoring forces, damping, and possibly external periodic driving.

**Common formula structures in this domain**:
- Simple harmonic oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x (linear restoring force)
- Damped harmonic oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v (linear restoring + linear damping)
- Driven damped oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * sin(c3 * t) (with periodic driving)
- Duffing oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 (cubic nonlinear restoring force)
- Damped Duffing oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * v (Duffing with damping)
- Driven Duffing oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * v + c3 * cos(c4 * t) (full Duffing equation)
- Van der Pol oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v * (1 + c2 * x**2) (nonlinear damping)
- Softening/hardening spring: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**2 + c2 * x**3 (general polynomial restoring force)
- Nonlinear damping: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * v * x**2 + c3 * v**3 (velocity-dependent nonlinear dissipation)
- Parametric oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x * (1 + c1 * cos(c2 * t)) (time-modulated stiffness)
- Coulomb + viscous damping: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * v + c2 * v * (v**2)**(-0.5) * v (mixed damping, effectively sign(v))
- Quintic Duffing: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * x**5 (higher-order nonlinear restoring force)
- General nonlinear oscillator: dv_dt = c0 * x + c1 * x**3 + c2 * v + c3 * x**2 * v + c4 * cos(c5 * t) (combined nonlinearities with driving)

**Formula structure heuristic features**:
- Oscillation damping envelope → Exponential decay envelope f(x)*exp(-c*Abs(x)) for symmetric damped oscillation, Gaussian envelope f(x)*exp(-c*x**2) for localized wave packets
- Saturation/clipping effects → Rational fraction x/(1+x), tanh; for variables with alternating positive/negative in oscillatory systems, selectively apply Abs
- Growth/decay → Exponential growth exp(c*t), Logistic 1/(1+exp(-c*x)), power law x**c
- Threshold/activation behavior → Max(x-c, 0), piecewise functions
- Coupling/cross effects → Product terms between variables x*y, power-law coupling x**c1 * y**c2
- Odd-power restoring forces (x, x³, x⁵) → symmetric double-well or hardening/softening potential energy landscape
- Velocity times even powers of position (v*x², v*x⁴) → amplitude-dependent damping characteristic of van der Pol-type oscillators
- Trigonometric functions of time (sin(c*t), cos(c*t)) → external periodic driving force; look for resonance phenomena
- Cubic position term with opposite sign to linear term (c0*x - c1*x³ with c0,c1>0) → double-well Duffing potential with bistability
- Product terms x*v → phase-space-dependent forces indicating energy exchange between kinetic and potential modes
- Even powers of x in restoring force (x²) → asymmetric potential, breaking spatial symmetry
- Odd powers of velocity (v³, v⁵) → nonlinear dissipation that preserves time-reversal symmetry breaking direction
- Absence of explicit time dependence → autonomous system; energy is conserved (if no v terms) or monotonically changes (with v terms)

============================================================
GT Equivalence Verification (PO7)
============================================================
GT: -alpha*v**c0 - beta*Abs(v)**c1 - omega0**c2*x**c3
Candidate formulas: 50

I need to determine if any candidate formula can be made equivalent to the GT:

$$-0.0458938827292319 \cdot v^3 - 1.28311219603499 \cdot x^3 - 0.729560991204844 \cdot |v|^{0.333333}$$

The GT has three terms: a $v^3$ term, an $x^3$ term, and an $|v|^{1/3}$ term. Note the crucial detail that one term uses $|v|$ (absolute value) while another uses $v$ (which for $v^3$ is the same as $|v|^3 \cdot \text{sign}(v)$).

The key challenge is that the GT contains $|v|^{1/3}$, which involves the absolute value function. None of the candidates explicitly use `Abs(v)`. The candidates use `v**c` terms, which for fractional exponents like $c=1/3$ would only be defined for $v \geq 0$ (or would be complex for $v < 0$), unlike $|v|^{1/3}$.

However, let me check if any candidate can still match. If we restrict to $v \geq 0$, then $|v|^{1/3} = v^{1/3}$, and a candidate with `v**c2` where $c2 = 1/3$ would work. But mathematically, for ALL values of $v$ (including negative), $v^{1/3}$ and $|v|^{1/3}$ are different functions.

Let me look for candidates that have exactly the right structure: $c \cdot x^3 + c \cdot v^3 + c \cdot v^{c}$ plus extra terms that can be zeroed out.

**Candidate 8**: `c0*x**3 + c1*v**c2 + c3*v**c4 + c5*v**3 + c6*v**2 + c7`
Set c0=-1.283, c5=-0.04589, c1=-0.7296, c2=1/3, c3=0, c6=0, c7=0. This gives: $-1.283x^3 - 0.7296v^{1/3} - 0.04589v^3$. This matches the GT if $v^{1/3} = |v|^{1/3}$, but these differ for $v < 0$.

**Candidate 9**: `c0*x**3 + c1*v**c2 + c3*v**3 + c4*v**c5 + c6*v**2 + c7*v + c8`

**Candidate 28**: `c0*x**3 + c1*v**3 + c2*v**c3 + c4*v**c5 + c6*sin(c7*v + c8)`

Actually, wait - in many computational systems and in real analysis, $|v|^{1/3}$ can be written as $(v^2)^{1/6}$. But none of the candidates have $v^2$ raised to a power.

No candidate can represent $|v|^{1/3}$ using only powers of $v$ (without absolute value) for all real $v$. The absolute value function introduces even symmetry in that term, which $v^c$ cannot replicate for non-even, non-integer exponents.

After careful analysis, none of the candidates can exactly match the GT for all values of $v$ (including negative values) because none can reproduce the $|v|^{1/3}$ term.
